I received it in the mail three days ago, and cracked it open immediately. It came with the amp, the wall charger, an extra battery, a USB cable for charging, and the in-line connector.<br /><br />First impression:<br />It's a well built device, very light, and seemed easy enough to use. Without charging it, I plugged it in and let it go. I listen to a lot of EDM (Glitch Hop, Drum and Bass, Downtempo, Breakbeat and a bit of Dub). I tried it out on a few tracks, changing the settings a couple times while they were playing. Some of the tracks seemed to be a bit bass-heavy and fuzzy no matter what the settings were at. I credit that to poor mixing and/or low bit-rate.<br /><br />Currently I'm using a set of ATH-M30's at home and some stock Samsung earbuds that came with my phone, on the go. Not the greatest set up, but they get the job done. With the M30's, the sound quality overall is not DRASTICALLY improved, but there is definitely a noticeable difference. With the earbuds however, it's hard to believe that it's the same pair. They go from being a flat, dull, and boring set to a set that you'd think cost upwards of $50-$60.<br /><br />I like that it's compact, and easy to carry. I currently have mine velcro'd to the back of my iPod. It adds to the size, no doubt, but it's nothing that isn't manageable.<br />I don't like that I feel like I'm gonna break it when I'm taking the back off to change the battery. Hasn't happened yet, and from the reviews I've read, I don't think it's something that can be expected. I also don't like that you can't use it when it's plugged into the charger. Other than that, I'd definitely recommend this product to anyone who is looking for an improvement in their listening experience. However I wouldn't suggest it to anyone who is looking for a top-notch pro-quality amp.

B0002KZAKS|5.0|I have been playing and singing off and on for close to 20 years and currently host an open mic at a local coffee shop. I have used lots of great equipment and mics including the oft mentioned sm57 and sm58. While I did not have either one of those to do an a/b comparison - I DID have 2 high-end (over $100 mics) to compare these too. One was an EV and the other a rather costly older Peavey. The signal was not quite as hot as the EV and Peavey, but far more resistant to feedback - even when turned up to match the hotter volumes of the other 2. Great value & highly recommended.

I might just turn the whole book over and read it again from the start.<br /><br />So well-written, with understated flair and savvy, and a deep understanding of the teenage mind. Even more aptly for me, he writes with a profound understanding of the mind of the parent of a teenager. The protagonist's wry observations of teenage behavior combined with his intense and protective parental love is a combination of emotions I have never encountered before in fiction. He breaks the rules of fiction in a way that only makes the book more compelling, such as casually dropping the name of the \\"real killer\\" in such a way that I'm pretty sure he's not lying, but still feel like I'm guessing. He plays with time like that, like a guy telling you his story over a cup of coffee. Landay is so interesting that I could read this book for days in utter joy even if nothing ever happened. He could get away with simply being entertaining because he is that good, but he doesn't; he is better than that, and the plot moves along with a thrilling crackle that reminds me of Lee Child. Landay has a wonderful mastery of language that gives me the same sense of wonder I get when I read poetry. For example (and this is off the top of my head), he describes a teenager \\"sloping\\" along; he describes the protagonist's rising anger by detailing the sense of autonomy and tension in the punching arm of the protagonist. This type of language and writing fills the book, using an economy of words that is truly poetry, but Landay's compelling writing makes it nearly impossible for me to take a breath and stand in wonder at the craftsmanship, because I just want so much to know what happens next.<br /><br />Read by Grover Gardener, who is pretty much my all-time favorite male narrator. He has a very gentlemanly and precise way of reading, which really brings out both the humor and the horror in this book.s it nearly impossible for me to take a breath and stand in wonder at the craftsmanship, because I just want so much to know what happens next.<br /><br />Read by Grover Gardener, who is pretty much my all-time favorite male narrator. B0076XDGZ0|5.0|Frequently, supergroups produce some of the least \\"super\\" and most underwhelming music out there. More often than not, we see great players and creative minds collaborating on albums with one another, only to discover, upon hearing these records, that none of the individual member's defining characteristics have been preserved.  Instead, these supergroups-- the Chickenfoots of the world-- produce music that's bland and colorless; that is simply \\"there\\"; that is, without a doubt, \\"less than the sum of its parts.\\"<br /><br />Thankfully, that is NOT the case with this new album. Instead, this record is some of the most inspired playing and most creative music that I've heard from any of the artists involved in quite some time. It is not watered down in the slightest, as supergroups tend to be. Nor can it be caricatured one way or the other; as \\"prog rock\\" or anything else. It defies labels. It is, simply put, five guys with unique attitudes toward music coming together to produce something genuinely fresh, new, and exciting. Sometimes it's prog, or metal, or 70's/80's rock, but listening to it you always get the sense that these five guys are playing this music and making it their own-- rather than coming down to from their expertise to play as studio-guys. Everyone shines here, and everyone's going all out. That maybe is why it's called \\"Flying Colors\\".<br /><br />Though I initially had my reservations that this album would simply become another Neal Morse solo project, that has not been the case. Neal's contributions are apparent, but the primary singer/songwriter here seems to be Casey McPhereson, who I sincerely hope will make this project the main outlet for his creativity henceforth.  Although, at this point, I'm not sure who is responsible for which songs, the songwriting here is top-notch. Though many songs deal with the same religious themes fans of Neal Morse will be familiar with, at no place is this album as overtly \\"biblical\\" as Neal's solo-work. The thematic range is much broader- there are love-songs, songs about hardship, songs about faith, and so on, and while I sense that these topics are dealt with in sort of a \\"christian\\" way, they are still portrayed universally enough for non-Christians to appreciate them. Casey's voice, heard most often on the album, is a nice parallel to the other voices in the group and compliments the music nicely.<br /><br />Mike Portnoy and Steve Morse are at the top of their game here-- though the latter can do no wrong, the former displays once again why progressive music is his home. Over the years, Mike Portnoy's best drumming has not been in Adrenaline Mob, A7X, or the heavier, post-Six Degrees DT albums he has played on. Rather, it has been in Transatlantic and Neal Morse. This album is no exception. Mike is at the top of this game here, and locks in with bassists David LaRue's grooving style perfectly. I think this is his best drumming in a long, long time. It is further proof that, where Portnoy is a just a minor-player in the metal world, he is a prophet in the dominion of prog rock. As for Steve Morse? His playing is astounding. Every note on the album sounds so \\"right\\", like he could not have played a better note, or done with more or less. He truly is a master of the craft.<br /><br />What makes Flying Colors so special is that it is not just Transatlantic + Dixie Dregs + Alpha Rev. It's something much different, and something new. \\"Kayla\\" makes us tear up, while \\"The Storm\\" liberates us, and the chorus to \\"Infinite Fire\\" will call to us when for weeks, even when we're not listening to it. It's an emotional and exciting album, and I sincerely hope that this project will be more than a one-off thing.tic range is much broader- there are love-songs, songs about hardship, songs about faith, and so on, and while I sense that these topics are dealt with in sort of a \\"christian\\" way, they are still portrayed universally enough for non-Christians to appreciate them. B003G4W49C|4.0|For the most part this book is entertaining.  Given its year of publication, I was surprised at how many of the things covered in this book remain relevant to this day.  One of the pleasantly surprising elements of the plot involves anonymous rhetoricians posting their thoughts on political philosophy and current events on the web, and watching their words reshape human society.  I got the feeling while reading this book that it could have been penned by someone writing within the last year or two.<br /><br />What I think is the overarching theme of the book is also something that remains relevant today.  Young children essentially coerced, through the circumstances of their births, into military service.  They are picked up from their homes, taken far away, and trained to fight a war that they are told is for the survival of the human race.  Once given the tools necessary to wage war, and wage it well, they realize that what that had been told was not necessarily the truth.  Essentially, the protagonist is tricked into doing things that he would not have chosen to do if he had known the entire story.  In the end, things turn towards a search for redemption.<br /><br />The parallels here should be obvious to anyone aware of the last ten years of American history, so I will not go into laborious detail.  I will simply say that I found those themes enjoyable, and extremely relevant to our times.<br /><br />In spite of that, I found parts of the book a bit cumbersome.  Too much time, I think, is devoted towards the battle school.  Once that is complete and Ender is ready to move into his real test, the finale and resolution of the story seem rushed, though somewhat satisfying.  I do not think the time in the battle school is unimportant, and do not look at this as a zero sum thing where elements of the story of Ender's training should be sacrificed in order to devote those pages to the climax and resolution, but I would liked to have seen more of those things towards the end.  (And I am dancing around here trying to avoid putting out any spoilers.)  Specifically, I do not like how Ender simply checks out and then has the events on Earth after his final battle related to him by another character.  I would like to have seen more of those events for myself, rather than have them summarized.  Based on a synopsis I have read of the second book, though, I think these things may be addressed there.<br /><br />Although I find this book entertaining, interesting, and worthy of a read, I have a hard time understanding why people proclaim it a classic.  Perhaps they read it when they were younger, as I did books like 1984, Brave New World, Animal Farm, etc. and remember it fondly as Ender's Game represents their first introduction to these themes.  It is a good book, I recommend it, and it is only going to grow in popularity as we approach its imminent movie adaptation (which is already being filmed).he end. The 3 and a half hour version explored in greater detail the relationships of the members of the submarine crew, their quarrels and their comaraderie, the first weeks of the submarine's uneventful search for British convoys and destroyers, and the ingenuity needed to both run the submarine and make the necessary repairs on it in the aftermath of being attacked by enemy (British) destroyers.  The five hour version stresses to an even greater extent these aspects of the film already evident in the three and a half hour version and portrays in an interesting manner the tedium of waiting to encounter the enemy vessels in the middle of the Atlantic.  However, the technical maneuvering of the ship and the repairs necessary to be done and the chit-chat amongs the crewmembers and officers are recorded in even greater detail.  Also in the five hour version, the viewer has the weird sense that he has become a member of the crew living day in and day out the 70 day journey of this German submarine's journey through the Atlantic, the straits of Gibraltar and into the Mediterranean Sea.  One has a greater understanding of how the German sailors personal hygiene and health is compromised by being cooped up in a submarine.  The five hour version has the disadvantage of being less likely to be viewed in one sitting.  One feels compelled to watch the remaining third on the following day.  In the three and a half hour version one finds the drama more intensified into one single totally absorbing sitting.<br /><br />The movie \\"Das Boot\\"'s merits consist in seeing the officers and crewmembers not as ardent Nazis, but as German mendoing their naval duties and thereby serving their country. Indeed, the stoic captain and his lieutenants in the movie speak disdainfully of Hitler's bombastic speeches and the fanatical nature of the Nazi movement. The crew comes across as human beings helplessly caught up in a conflict that they did not create.  At one point they even show their admiration of their enemy (the British navy and convoy) when they sing \\"It's a long way to Tipperary.\\" The German crew even feels remorse when it can not rescue the few remaining British sailors who jump off from their oil tanker in flames that has been torpedoed by the German submarine.  This movie is an excellent expression of what life would be like aboard a German submarine during World War II.  And one can feel great sympathy for the doomed German sailors who struggle to survive against a British foe who is becoming less vulnerable both in the air and on the water because of their increasing sophisticated use of radar and sophisticated maneuvering tactics that make attacks by the German U-Boat wolf-packs increasingly less successful and more treacherous.  The plight of this German crew seems to mirror the turning of the tide of World War II against the Germans.

What I most appreciated about this book is that it opened up my eyes to the struggles of addicts of all kinds. This is not an easy book - I am a fast, voracious reader, and it took me several months... had to read it in short bursts with a notebook in hand just to keep track of what was going on - and I'm still not sure, in many parts, what DID happen...but there are images seared into my head from this book that I will never forget (some of them I wish I could). Most of all, I appreciate this work for giving me compassion and insights into the struggles of the kind of people whom I had previously written off. This is a book I plan on rereading every few years. My advice: 1) don't skip the footnotes (2) give it until page 150 before giving up (3) realize if one storyline is boring you, another one is just around the corner. Good luck!

The one bright spot in his life as a child was listening to his grandfather's stories.  These stories were presented to him as truth, but as he grew, he began to lose his innocent faith in his grandfather.  He outgrows the magic of these stories, much like we all grow to realize that Santa Claus and his magical elves aren't real.  But what if, in following your heart and the last words of your dying grandfather, you discovered that the magical stories were real?<br />I felt such empathy for a grandfather who lived a double life in solitude, with nobody who believed in him.  I felt sorry for Jacob, who understood his grandfather too late to let him know. 1400034205|5.0|W.Somerset Maughham's 1944 novel \\"The Razor's Edge\\" is a challenging but engrossing novel, still in print.  Maugham is his own narrator in a tale that follows the lives of a handful of characters in the years between the two World Wars.  In Maugham's own words, it is the story of their separate paths to success, but that success is defined quite differently for each...<br /><br />At the center of the story is Larry Durrell, a young American aviator who comes out of the First World War with PTSD and a driving need to find inner peace.  Larry's quest will take him on an extended journey through Europe and ultimately to India.  Along the way, he will be engaged to two very different women.  Isabel has a desperate love for Larry, but an equal need for a wealthy lifestyle and social prominence; Sophie is a deep but wounded soul whom Larry will try to rescue.  Gray is the decent and conventional guy who competes for the beautiful Isabel; Elliot is Isabel's snobbish but doting uncle, a fixture of the Paris social scene.<br /><br />Maugham's narrator interacts with the principal characters at long intervals; the changes in their lives become apparent with each meeting.  The narrative is laced with much acute social commentary and some fascinating vignettes, especially of inter-war Paris.  The characters are living, breathing creations, attractive to the reader in spite of their very human faults.  The punch line of the novel is saved for the concluding pages; the ending is unconventional and may be unsatisfying for some readers.  \\"The Razor's Edge\\" is very highly recommended as one of Maugham's best novels, with a theme still relevant to today's readers.

B00335EQ0E|5.0|The 2-disc \\"Un-Anniversary\\" DVD is a wonderful set that complements the wonderful film with a treasure trove of interesting bonus content for the Disney enthusiasts and film historians among us.  It's a thoroughly satisfying DVD, though I can't offer a comparison to other versions that have been released.<br /><br />THE MOVIE:<br /><br />I loved watching the Disney animated films on VHS when I was a kid, but as I got older I tended to favor the more recent Disney hits (\\"Aladdin\\", \\"The Lion King\\", etc.) over the original classics.  I hadn't enjoyed \\"Alice in Wonderland\\" as much as, say, \\"Peter Pan\\" or even \\"101 Dalmatians\\", so the tape gathered dust over the years and my recollection of the movie got buried in my mind.  It had been so long since I'd seen \\"Alice\\" that when I watched this DVD I found there were scenes and songs that I'd forgotten all about, so it was like viewing the film with a fresh perspective.<br /><br />And I must say that this movie is great.  It's delightfully surreal and whimsical, and it's filled with fun characters and memorable scenes.  The animation by Disney's team is, unsurprisingly, fantastic and the art, styled after Mary Blair's graphic sensibilities, is beautiful.  The movie may not pack the wallop of some of Disney's higher-rated masterpieces, but it's a wonderful animated adventure.<br /><br />THE BONUS FEATURES:<br /><br />Although this DVD doesn't contain a full-length audio commentary, the \\"Reflections On Alice\\" featurette is a satisfying retrospective on Lewis Carroll's stories, Walt Disney's efforts to bring them to the screen, and the impact of the 1951 film.<br /><br />\\"Operation Wonderland\\" is a fascinating vintage promotional featurette that takes a behind-the-scenes look at the making of \\"Alice in Wonderland\\" at the Disney animation studio.  Walt Disney leads narrator James Melton through the studio, showing how animation cels are photographed, how sequences are storyboarded, and how animators reference live-action performances.  There's some priceless footage of vaudeville comedian Ed Wynn (the voice of the Mad Hatter), Kathryn Beaumont (Alice), and Jerry Colonna (the March Hare) acting out the tea party scene on a soundstage while the animators sketch away.  (Improvised dialogue from these sessions was incorporated into the final film.)<br /><br />A more awkward curiosity is the half-hour excerpt from an episode of The Fred Waring Show, which promotes the music of the Disney film, featuring Kathryn Beaumont and Sterling Holloway (the Cheshire Cat) along with a chorus dressed in disturbingly crude costumes.<br /><br />The DVD also contains several demo recordings of songs that did not make it into the final film, as well as an early storyboard sequence of the scene from the book with the Duchess and her baby (which didn't make it into the film either).  The extensive art gallery features concept art from some different artists, including a showcase of Mary Blair's beautifully stylized work, along with some behind-the-scenes photos of Walt Disney and his animators.<br /><br />We even get two of Walt's television introductions to the film (a black & white one from 1954 and a very similar color one from 1964) and two theatrical trailers.<br /><br />Disc One contains the classic (and totally relevant) Mickey Mouse cartoon \\"Thru the Mirror\\", and Disc Two contains the early 1920s live-action/animation hybrid short \\"Alice's Wonderland\\".<br /><br />\\"One Hour in Wonderland\\" is a family-friendly holiday television special from 1950 featuring Walt Disney, Kathryn Beaumont, Edgar Bergen (with his pals Charlie McCarthy and Mortimer Snerd, of course), and Bobby Driscoll.  It shows clips from Mickey Mouse cartoons and Disney films like \\"Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs\\", \\"Song of the South\\" (Uncle Remus!), and, you guessed it, the soon-to-be-released \\"Alice in Wonderland\\".  (The black & white program has been reconstructed for DVD to include movie clips in full color.)  There's also a neat appearance by the Firehouse Five Plus Two, the dixieland band made of Disney animators.<br /><br />For the younger crowd, Disc One contains interactive games and activities (\\"Virtual Wonderland Party\\" and \\"Adventures in Wonderland\\") which can be played using the remote control.  So there's enough extras to satisfy just about anyone.<br /><br />THE VERDICT:<br /><br />\\"Alice in Wonderland\\" is a unique fantasy from the glory days of Walt Disney animation.  It's enchanting to kids and adults alike, and really is a work of art.  The 2010 \\"Un-Anniversary Edition\\" DVD is a delightful package for anyone who loves the film and a welcome addition to any collection.  Definitely worth buying if you're interested, although this version may be outdated by more recent re-releases.

1933633867|4.0|The argument in this book is brilliant, taking the reader on a thoroughly anthropological tour of the oft ambivalent functions and forms of debt that prompts a serious revision of neoclassical economic dogma. Some of the history, however, is drawn out, unnecessarily detailed, and in one section irrelevant (an editing error perhaps?). The first half of the book contains most of the theoretical claims which are then backed up in the second half, but it really gets quite bogged down in parts, and the concluding chapter could have been greatly expanded upon.<br /><br />Whilst offering food for thought on the importance of debt as an explanatory framework of history, Graeber's argument is a little one sided. It seems to me difficult to imagine a modern nation state, as opposed to a small community, in which exchange could be based on anything but an impersonal medium such as money. His depiction of credit systems therefore, whilst fascinating, seems to have little bearing on the modern world. I also feel that Graeber fails to appreciate the importance of impersonal money, debt and credit for innovation, if not explicitly, implicitly by its absence. It is hard to imagine, for example, how programmes requiring massive input of human and material capital could have been undertaken more equitably without money.<br /><br />I lived with an indigenous (and of course, anarchist) community in the Atlantic Forest to conduct fieldwork, and whilst a beautiful, humble and caring society that we have much to learn from, they have not been to the moon, they did not invent the internet, and they are heavily reliant upon modern medicine, and i think what is missing from Graeber's book is an explicit discussion of his view of 'the good society'. If we are to have an advanced technological society I think we have to take the rough with the smooth. It is only if we accept the anarchist form of living that we all know Graeber supports that his claims have any real political purchase, and I'mnot convinced that given the brute facts of the modern world that this is a realistic or even desirable state of affairs. I'm not dismissing these claims entirely, and I would love to be convinced otherwise, but left implicit I think Graeber's work will fall into the intellectually stimulating but practically not useful category, which is a shame.

The United States of America is no more. In its place, is a nation-state called Panem, occupying much of North America. Its capital is in the Rocky Mountains, and the rest of the nation-state is divided into thirteen regions -- or at least it was. Sometime around seventy-five years before the story begins, the thirteen regions attempted to rise up and secede from Panem, but they were defeated. In fact, the thirteenth region was nuked and is no more. As a legacy of this squelched rebellion, the Capitol holds an event called The Hunger Games each year, where one boy and one girl from each of the twelve remaining regions are drafted to fight in a televised fight-to-the-death. The purpose of this event is twofold: 1) Keep the decadent citizens of the Capitol entertained, and 2) To remind the citizens of the twelve regions that they are utterly powerless to in the face of the Capitol's might.<br /><br />The world in which this novel takes place is a dystopian one. We mostly know about District 12, where the protagonist Katniss Everdeen lives. It is a coal-mining district in the Appalachians. The people there, and presumably in the other eleven regions, are essentially serfs -- unable to travel to other regions or even leave the fenced-in borders of their own region (legally, at least). They are hungry and starvation is a regular cause of death. Technology in the Capitol is advanced, but Katniss has never even been in a car, and there are rolling electrical blackouts. District 12 is nominally governed by a mayor, but the real powers that be are the \\"Peacekeepers\\" from the Capitol. Luckily for Katniss and her fellow District-12ers, these Peacekeepers are relatively benevolent and turn a blind eye to the poaching and black-market activity that keeps the district's economy afloat. We learn in the book that the citizens of other districts are not so lucky, and their Peacekeepers are more brutal. Perhaps this is a function of District 12 being far from the Capitol.<br /><br />As you can probably guess, Katniss ends up as a participant in The Hunger Games. The story up until this point had me rapt, and once the games begin, things get even more interesting. I devoured the book in a few days, and I loved it the whole way through -- until the last few chapters. At this point, the \\"young adultness\\" of the book revealed itself, as the author simply made things far too easy on the characters. Katniss never really has to make a tough moral choice; everything just falls into place. The author could have explored some deeper moral issues here, but she took the easy way out instead. This is why I have to give The Hunger Games four stars instead of five.<br /><br />Ultimately, The Hunger Games is about the evil of a remote central government and the cruelty of government-sanctioned violence. It can be viewed as an examination of our \\"reality TV\\" culture, mixed with our obsession with war -- so long as we send other people's kids to do the fighting. Violence here is not glorified -- it is dealt with realistically and shown to be horrible and horrifying. I just wish the author would have given her young readers more to think about with a more plausible and challenging ending.ominally governed by a mayor, but the real powers that be are the \\"Peacekeepers\\" from the Capitol. Luckily for Katniss and her fellow District-12ers, these Peacekeepers are relatively benevolent and turn a blind eye to the poaching and black-market activity that keeps the district's economy afloat. The game progresses in a fairly linear fashion, with the player largely seeking items/clues that remove barriers and reveal new items/clues that remove other barriers. There is one \\"classic\\" puzzle in the game, and the lack of any others gives this game a particularly low replay value. This isn't really a big deal in a sprawling game like Myst, but in a game like this, which is probably winnable in 5 minutes or less if the player already knows what to do, it poses a big problem. It will deliver no more than a few hours of fun on the first playthrough, after which it will be largely useless. Contrast this with a title like Plants vs. Zombies, Fruit Ninja, or Angry Birds, which for a few more dollars offer almost endless replayability, and The Secret of Grisly Manor starts to look a little sad.<br /><br />The primary selling-point here seems to be the graphics, which are admittedly better than those of the free Flash games out there - though not by much, really. Lots of people will probably enjoy this game on its own merits, but I would suggest that experienced puzzlers and adventure gamers spend their coveted $0.99 elsewhere.

B007MS5B6Q|3.0|The inaugural season of The Walking Dead raised enormous expectations with its balanced blend of horror, action, and serious adult drama, coupled with high production values and hints that the series would play out on an epic scale. Season Two (aka \\"Escape from Hershel's Boring Farm\\") started to systematically undermine those expectations and change the very nature of the show.<br /><br />The action and horror elements quickly receded into the background after the brilliant herd sequence in the season opener. Every drama needs rising and falling tension, but the show dramatically slowed its pace, focusing on life at a Georgia farm, and morphing into a rather overwrought soap opera--a sort of \\"One Life to Live\\" with the occasional token zombie. The show's setting became so confined that it was easy to forget that there had even been a total breakdown of society; Season Two just wanted us to take it on faith that there was something scary over the hill. And without the horror elements, the show in essence becomes an American version of the BBC's post-apocalyptic series Survivors.<br /><br />Some rambling, repetitive writing and mediocre direction didn't help matters. (The lack of Frank Darabont's input this season is striking.) Those great \\"Walking Dead Moments,\\" where a character is stuck in a horrific situation and forced to make a gut-wrenching decision came less frequently or became so predictable that they ceased to make much impact.<br /><br />But stick it out: by the end of the season, things are mostly back in swing, and the show we came to love from Season One appears to be more or less back on track. There are some shocking and heartbreaking dramatic moments, as well as unexpected deaths. By the final episode, the group is again on the run, fighting to survive both the walkers and their own worse natures. (The show is at its best when it balances both those threats.) Now, if the creators and directors can balance the ethical/psychological elements with a renewed integration of action and zombie horror, we'll be in for a thrilling Season Three. If it's just a group of frightened, selfish, confused people bickering in a prison, it will be a requiem for what might have been.<br /><br />In Season Two, the makeup design is still superb, but the visual effects are still hit or miss, with too much of the spraying blood looking like dodgy, 300-style CGI. Audio effects are as comically over the top as in the first season: every time a walker is killed, it sounds like someone whacking a giant rotten watermelon with a sledgehammer. Once again, the show's comic book roots show, with characters performing utter miracles of marksmanship with the greatest of ease.<br /><br />(Btw, what's up with T-Dog? Is he a \\"Red Shirt\\" the producers forgot to kill? The poor character had maybe three lines in the entire second season. Apparently he spent most of his time snoozing in a hammock behind the farmhouse. Season Three will tell...)<br /><br />As for the Blu-ray/DVD set itself: be wise and wait for the inevitable Gold Platinum Extra-Special Super-Deluxe Edition that includes more extras than the initial release--as was done with the various releases of the first season.

B006MGC3VK|3.0|I've been with OSI since their eponymous debut album, and have enjoyed most of what I have heard so far. I own their entire discography on CD and they're in constant rotation in my car, especially \\"Blood,\\" which is my favorite (including this release).<br /><br />These songs offer a bit more variety in tempo and theme than their previous works, which seemed a bit more bent on giving the listener more of a heavy metal experience. I feel like Kevin Moore has finally merged Chroma Key (my favorite Kevin Moore project) with the former, heavier OSI for this album. It does not disappoint. Many tracks are mellow, like the melancholic \\"For Nothing\\" or \\"Indian Curse,\\" while other tracks follow the OSI we already know well - the kind of song that, if heard while driving, will invariably result in a speeding ticket, like \\"Enemy Prayer\\" or \\"Big Chief II.\\" And we even have a \\"progressive\\" exploration in the mix with the closer \\"Invisible Men.\\"<br /><br />Why three stars? First, while the record is enjoyable, no single song instantly grabs me and calls me back like \\"Go\\" or \\"The Escape Artist\\" did from previous albums. This doesn't mean I hate it; it just means I've heard OSI make something more catchy in previous albums. Another reason I docked a star is due to the mastering. This album teeters on the edge of clipping from over-compression and poor brickwalling, which is nearly ubiquitous anymore (especially in rock genres). I posted the official dynamic range results on the justficeforaudio.org DR Database moments ago, and it doesn't look pretty. In fact, the song \\"Invisible Men\\" often yields that tell-tale \\"snap crackle pop\\" you hear in your speakers/headphones when the waveform is clipped; specifically during the first and second crescendos. It will probably be worse for those of you who choose to download the lossy mp3s or AAC files (I'm listening straight off the CD using high-end components and equipment). CDs can only take so much loudness before \\"topping out,\\" and this CDis constantly riding on the edge of the CD envelope, though never GRATUITOUSLY crossing the line. I also felt that the drum mixing was poor - Gavin's bass drum often drowns out the sound around it for a few milliseconds following each drum kick, which has also become common in the metal genre (and, strangely, is one of the trademarks for whoever used to mix former OSI drummer Mike Portnoy's drum kit too). With that in mind, I've heard clipping/compression worse than this, just not from OSI.<br /><br />All in all, if you're already an OSI fan, you'll want to pick this up and give it a spin. If you're just getting into them, try \\"Blood\\" or \\"Free\\" before you go here.

B000UR9QIK|5.0|George A. Romero's Dawn of the Dead is an unlikely masterpiece.  It had a very modest budget and only a couple months to complete.  Since they filmed in a real mall, they could only shoot late at night; making it tough for all personnel.  If that isn't bad enough, there wasn't enough money for any big name actors or actresses.  With so many odds against it, somehow the film found a path to success.  Word-of-mouth clearly had a lot of impact, because the film finally quenched horror fans thirst for gore. The prior success of Night of the Living Dead also had an affect on the success of Dawn of the Dead.  The film works well in many genres and it has a great script.  Dawn of the Dead is a spellbinding masterpiece that visually shows the labor of love and all the blood, sweat and tears that went into the film..<br /><br />Multiple genres used in the film creates a broader fan base.  Dawn of the Dead uses horror and fantasy as a foundation.  Essentially, zombies are very eerie.  Unlike vampires, zombies have a very authentic notion to them.  The fact that they contain no ridiculous characteristics, fabricates a rational fear.  In essence, George A. Romero invented the \\"flesh eating\\" zombie (inspired by I Am Legend), which is a scarier idea than the typical mind controlled-voodoo zombies in earlier films.  Dawn of the Dead is brilliant because it uses themes of science and religious conviction as devices of horror.  It's never confirmed whether if it's a virus like in Resident Evil, or if it's the end times suggested by a character in the film named Peter, who said \\"When there's no more room in Hell, the dead will walk the earth.\\"  Behind the dreadfulness, a luxurious fantasy is at hand.  Once you let go of the idea that zombies look like people, the film fabricates a fun proposal; survive by any means necessary.  It provides a fantasy that people can actually use guns to survive, as opposed to plainly murder in cold blood.  In the film, a bunch of rednecks and military soldiers use the scenario as a zombie hunting game, but the main characters find a fantasy of their own.  Imagine going to the mall and getting everything for free. Is it worth risking your life fighting through the hordes of zombies?<br /><br />The film has a really smart script. All the characters are relatable and dynamic. Stephen is a soldier.  He has a quick wit, bubbly personality, and gets along with everybody.  At the beginning of the film he's very rational, but when he gets too comfortable with the situation, that's when he takes a dynamic change for the worst.  His foolish actions got him bitten; eventually he died.  Peter is an African American soldier.  He is even-tempered, reserved, and looked upon as knowing the answers.  He is probably the least dynamic character; however, his friendship with Roger is dynamic.  Francine is the only female among this particular group of survivors.  At the beginning of the film, she's very overlooked; by the end of the film, her importance was vital.  Roger is the most dynamic character, and the most important character.  He's Francine's boyfriend, and Stephen's best friend.  He's also the only one who knows how to navigate a helicopter.  Eventually, he teaches Francine.  As far as him being dynamic; he was perceived as the weakest character.  His change was so gradual, that as an viewer, we realized it after he turned into a zombie.  Unlike George A. Romero's other films in the Dead series, this one had an emotional attachment to the characters.  It's also attention-grabbing that an African American was portrayed as the lead character.  Besides the script containing interesting character, it also contains an interesting plot delivery system.  The characters didn't really have any plans, apart from surviving.  Instead of characters following a certain predictable plot, the plot followed the characters.  That's very effective when it comes to keeping the viewers' interest.<br /><br />It's safe to say that Dawn of the Dead is George A. Romero's magnum opus. It's like Apocalypse Now, but in a urban setting and with zombies.  I'm a critic about everything, and there's nothing I say to criticize this film.  With a modest budget, and limited resources, this is the best film is could possibly be.  As far as the zombie genre is concerned, only a few films have inhaled the tour de force \\"aroma\\" of Dawn of the Dead.  Films such as 28 Days Later, Planet Terror and the Dawn of the Dead remake.  But George A. Romero's Dawn of the Dead, made in 1978, still sets high standards for films in any genre.ad is George A. Romero's magnum opus. As far as the zombie genre is concerned, only a few films have inhaled the tour de force \\"aroma\\" of Dawn of the Dead.  Films such as 28 Days Later, Planet Terror and the Dawn of the Dead remake.  But George A. Romero's Dawn of the Dead, made in 1978, still sets high standards for films in any genre.

The pilot is so good Michael Chiklis (Vic) had to take a shower after first screening it. It follows a new, experimental station opened in the gang-infested, fictional neighborhood of Farmington.  In a departure from the standard police procedural approach, instead of a single focus the show follows a couple uniformed cops (the weakest portion), the politically ambitious police chief, two homicide detectives, and most of all Vic and his Strike Team.  It takes elements from different shows--Gangs + Law & Order style murder and sex crime investigations + beat cops--and puts them together.  All interact at times, and stories and investigations often span episodes.  There are some ripped-from-the-headlines storylines, but The Shield doesn't go to that well nearly as often as Law & Order<br /><br />The Strike Team is a four-man anti-gang unit operating in essence autonomously under the unquestioned authority of Vic.  They are the sort of cops whose absolute belief in their own superiority to criminals and the righteousness of their mission allows them to pursue justice in a brutally effective manner, all while making a little extra cash on the side.  Some of the things Vic and his team do on The Shield are shocking, but if you've read much about the Rampart scandal, you know what actually happened was just as outrageous.  The real difference is that while the cops involved in the Rampart scandal were under little to no supervision, Vic and his team are under constant pressure from their captain.  But that creates a wonderful inner-conflict for the characters that aren't members of the Strike Team.  They hate Vic, but not as much as they hate the criminals they both fight.  And Vic is too good to ignore.<br /><br />The Shield pushes the envelope for basic cable.  It may not be as violent or feature as much nudity as an HBO drama, but it certainly squeezes as much past the censors as it could have been expected to.  To put it another way, if you showed it to me and told me it was an HBO drama I would not have expressed surprise.  It's shot in a voyeuristic style that gives the show a certain realism.<br /><br />The Shield has a great ensemble cast, but Michael Chiklis as Vic makes the show.<br /><br />DVD extras: Apparently there is commentary on every episode (there is no way I'm watching that many commentary tracks). 0451196716|5.0|ON BACHMAN:<br />Stephen King did not write this book, Richard Bachman did. What I mean by this is that Stephen King didn't just throw on a different name and call it good; he actually wrote in such a way that is different that how he writes in almost every way. There elements that remain the same, sure, such as character swearing. But, you gotta remember, up until this point, Stephen King had only released Carrie, 'salem's Lot, The Shining, Night Shift, The Stand . . . and then this book, with no supernatural elements--no wonder he had people fooled (at least from the start of Bachman's career).<br /><br />PREMISE:<br />One hundred kids from around a near future United States volunteer in a walk-a-thon until only one kid stands. If any of the walkers go under four miles per hour, they get warned; if they get warned three times, they get \\"the ticket.\\" What do you think the ticket it? Well, it isn't good. The ticket is what Robert Downey Jr.'s character sold in Tropic Thunder: a bullet. And to the head, of course. The last young boy still walking gets the PRIZE. The premise is as simple as that.<br /><br />ANALOGY OF WAR:<br />Why would young boys volunteer to walk in such a death race that they would likely die? Honestly, I don't know, but Stephen King was trying to make an analogy between the kids volunteering for the Long Walk (for their country) and going the service and going to war (for their country). Of course it is an antiwar message--and maybe Vietnam was the main vein that King drawing his creative juices from, because Vietnam was quite pointless--or so he believes (and so do I).<br /><br />Essentially it draws similarities between how when soldiers first enlist, they're eager and ready to go, ready to take care of business, ready to go in guns a-blazing; or maybe they thought it was just going be an easy walk on the beach and when they were done, their school would be paid for. And then, when they get summoned for war or a battle or their unit gets attacked, they probably had the same feelings that the Long Walkers did . . . why did I volunteer?<br /><br />But don't get me wrong, it's not so thick in this political analogy that it cannot be enjoyed if you don't share the same political point of view, because, in the end, this is a book a entertainment and horror and it's never preachy. But, where it's not trying to be preachy, it cannot help but be a sermon.<br /><br />The sermon is this: why are you doing this potentially deadly thing? Just why . . . that's all it asks.<br /><br />HOW IT COMPARES:<br />I've been recommended the heck out of this book (along with Battle Royale) with the people who've liked The Hunger Games, but a lot of those \\"young people\\" are addicted to the flavor of the month fads and won't take a risk with an older, more established writer, either because he's too popular, or too old. But, as someone who has read The Hunger Games, The Long Walk is the better book because it's better written and it never tries to suck up to the reader and he's not afraid to piss off the reader.<br /><br />And it's because of this: Richard Bachman is not such a nice guy.ed, they probably had the same feelings that the Long Walkers did . . . why did I volunteer?<br /><br />But don't get me wrong, it's not so thick in this political analogy that it cannot be enjoyed if you don't share the same political point of view, because, in the end, this is a book a entertainment and horror and it's never preachy. B000005HDT|5.0|As far back as the mid 1950's musicians such as Art Blakey's Jazz Messengers became intent on bringing jazz back to the African American community. In their time they by and large succeeded. Blues and gospel eventually began to lead into whole new styles of music such as R&B and soul as the 60's came in. Those in jazz who took to the new musics in some way did very well. Such as those who innovated in the soul jazz movement. By the time the 70's rolled around Donald Byrd was not only still an active musician but also an important music educator at Howard University where he'd soon put together a group known as the Blackbyrds,probably based on the title of this album. Recorded in 1972 but released the following year this recording found Byrd under the realization of the changes in the broader taste of the African American public by this point. Especially those bought on by the advent of Motown. This began his association with the Mizell brothers Fonce and Larry along with members of The Crusader-all of whom had played and written for different Motown acts. In particular The Jackson 5. So that influence was important to what happened here.<br /><br />  Now this album is not merely some heavy polyrhythmic jazz-funk exercise. This music is heavily based in pop/R&B. That means that melody,verses and choruses are a huge part this music almost more so than jazz soloing. The first two songs are in the the eight minute range. But with the advent of cinematic soul that had value too. \\"Flight Time\\" continues to build throughout the song never really arriving at anything. But that's the whole point. It stays on this terrific major key melody from beginning to end. The title song has a heftier groove with the use of moog synthesizer adding much of the weight.  The majority of the songs here such as \\"Love's So Far Away\\",\\"Mr.Thomas\\",\\"Sky High\\" and \\"Where Are We Going\\" actually have a lot more in common with certain types of pop/soul of the time than hardcore jazz-funk. Again melody and breezy grooves with a lot of wah wah and electric piano solos are the order of the day on these tunes. Byrd and the band are back to the heavy funk however on \\"Slop Jar Blues\\". Here the moog comes back into play and Byrd himself does a bit of singing as well. It's one of the heaviest grooves here in terms of funk.<br /><br />  Needless to say this album met with much the same fate as most jazz-funk of the time did critically. Writers of this era have just never seemed to deal with anything outside West Coast cool and be-bop as having any relevance to jazz. Most of the time they simply missed the point of this. But in later years hip-hoppers didn't as many,especially Madlib/Yesterday's New Quintet,found new ways of re-imagining grooves from albums like this after the turn of the millennium. Of course even that being the case,it's important this music be recognized on it's own terms too. It always seemed as if musicians are often a little more ahead of the game in terms of the audience's needs than writers and critics are. Especially black musicians. There's a strong futurist type creative thread running through that cultural pool. That thought process that links the past to the present by way of a possible future. And that might be where the scism between writer and artist exists. All too often a music writer will tend to sentimentalize the past and perceive any unfamiliar musical ideas as threatening and even dangerous to their perception of art. On this album whatever sounds Byrd is making draws the listener right into the music. So on it's creative intention,it's an enormously influential success.y and breezy grooves with a lot of wah wah and electric piano solos are the order of the day on these tunes. While Casey McPherson handles most of the lead vocals.<br /><br />Is this a new Transatlantic? Well, not really since the emphasis here is more on the songwriting, less on long suitelike compositions. Recalling 80s groups like Mr.Mister, Toto or Trevor Rabin era Yes. And actually almost every song is a winner with memorable hooklines, clever lyrics and definitely hit potential. But at the same time the album is full of elegant musical details, odd metres, strange breaks, magnificent soloes. Everything to satisfy the musos and the tech freaks also.<br /><br />Quite a music lovers dream and wonderful an album like this is still being made in 2012!

There are so many different sub-plots and so many moral crossroads, the books suck you in and don't ever let go. Morality is a highly flexible concept, and it is approached by each character in their own fashion without judgement - Larsson is very good at simply telling their stories and letting the reader make their own call, while managing to be clear on his own stance on things. In all my reading this stands out as one of the most original, complex and engrossing story I've ever found. I was devastated to learn he had died, as finding an author who can write such depth and insight into characters plus give us a complex plot melding such a variety of story lines is so rare.  This series as a whole is right up in my top 10 with Robin Hobb (Assassin trilogy/Mad Ships trilogy/Fool trilogy), The Stand by Steven King and Presumed Innocent by Scott Turow.

078324228X|4.0|The Hurricane does a great job enthralling the audience with Rubin Carter and his boxing career and causing people to have an emotional connection to Rubin \\"Hurricane\\" Carter's racist and unjust situation (his multiple imprisonments). The movie understandably changes some details of his life to paints a clear picture of who is wrong, and who is right; the wrong being the white detective and the right being Rubin Carter and his Canadian pals.<br /><br />Some details of his life, which were altered in the movie, were that in the movie, 11-year-old Rubin Carter throws a bottle at a child molester to protect the other kids. But Rubin Carter was in fact 14 years old and had stolen $55 dollars and a watch from a man. Also, Rubin Carter was seen returning from war as a successful soldier but in truth, he was released after 21 months after receiving an \\"Undesirable\\" discharge for being constantly AWOL. Finally, Rubin Carter was seen in the movie sitting confidently in the front seat the night of the murder while in reality, he was either hiding in the back seats or lying down while two other men sat in front.<br /><br /> These detail changes may seem miniscule and I believe they are because even though the movie doesn't truly represent the kind of person Rubin Carter was, his situation and the injustice done upon him were still shown and (I think) portrayed in a way that the viewers could understand and also relate. The movie on its own is a great entertainment but one should take caution if they want to learn about Rubin Carter's life.

B00756GKBA|4.0|For me, the release of a new Madonna album is always a big deal.  I've been a fan all my life, but really started following her career during the 90's.  That era, to me, is my favorite Madonna era.  Albums like Erotica, Bedtime Stories, Ray of Light and Music showed a variety and creativity that few pop artists had.  The 2000's were not particularly kind to Madonna.  American Life was practically ignored despite being a decent (albeit preachy) record.  Confessions on a Dance Floor was a shining bright spot during this time before dropping the bomb that was 2008's atrocious Hard Candy.  That album, while it was somewhat decent as a hip-hop-flavored record, was not very good as a Madonna record.  Madonna seemed forced during that record, and similarly I had to force myself to try and enjoy it.  And while I occasionally put that record on for a spin, it doesn't hold a candle to her other albums.<br /><br />Now it's 2012, and Madonna has unleashed her latest album upon the world.  MDNA has a lot of \\"firsts\\": it's her first album with a new record label, it's her first album of new material since releasing a career-spanning greatest hits album (Celebration), and the first album in a new Madonna decade, so to speak.  In the four years since Hard Candy, lots has happened to Madonna's personal life.  The biggest event is undoubtedly her divorce from ex-husband Guy Ritchie, and that above all else takes center stage here.  After a handful of playful dance numbers, Madonna gets down to the nitty gritty and delivers her most personal album since 1989's Like a Prayer (which, incidentally, was also a divorce record).  Helping her this time around is a number of electronica-infused producers such as Martin Solveig, Benny Benassi and her longtime collaborator William Orbit.  The result is an album that brings Madonna back to that level of brilliance and musical ingenuity that we saw in the 90's.  MDNA is every bit as cutting-edge and musically relevant as you would expect from theQueen of Pop.  And while Madonna curiously released the two weakest songs as singles so far, I can assure you that the rest of the album is miles above \\"Give Me All Your Luvin'\\" and \\"Girl Gone Wild\\".  Now I don't routinely do track-by-track reviews, but if any album requires one, it's this one.<br /><br />Girl Gone Wild - The album opens with a soft prayer similar to the Act of Contrition before blasting into this club-thumper.  Sadly, Benassi's production is as plain as it comes and Madonna's lyrics about as commonplace as they get.  Still a nice dance song, though it's not something that adequately befits Madonna.  Not the worst song, but not the best either.<br /><br />Gang Bang - Now THIS is the Madonna we've been waiting to hear!  Despite the pornographic-sounding title, the song isn't about sex at all.  It's a dark, pulsing revenge fantasy where Madonna envisions killing her lover.  The beat is strong, loud, and never lets up save for a fantastic dubstep breakdown in the middle...all before culminating in a rage-fueled finale.  This is an absolutely stellar track and a real highlight on this album.<br /><br />I'm Addicted - A club-ready love song filled with swirling production that beckons you to the dance floor.  Could be compared to Music's \\"Impressive Instant\\", and is just as memorable.  Love it.<br /><br />Turn Up The Radio - If fans have anything to say about it, this will be the next single.  Elation rules supreme here in this happy and carefree dance track.  This deserves to be played at top volume as you drive your car with the windows down on a clear summer's day!<br /><br />Give Me All Your Luvin' - This is by far the black sheep of the album.  It doesn't fit in with the rest of the album at all, and as MDNA's first single it might give listeners the wrong idea.  It's a cute, catchy pop song, but lacks the brilliance of the rest of the album.<br /><br />Some Girls - This one could also be a single.  Very distorted vocals with a strong, prominent and heavy dance beat.  Madonna references how \\"other girls\\" can be, but how that's not how she is.<br /><br />Superstar - A tender, upbeat love song.  Simple production underneath simple \\"Oh la la, you're my superstar, oh la la love the way that you are.\\"  It's like an updated version of something you'd hear on the Like a Virgin or True Blue albums.  Madonna absolutely makes it work!<br /><br />I Don't Give A - A hard-hitting confessional and assertive song, this is another highlight.  Madonna half-raps the lyrics as she details a day in her life before Nicki Minaj perfectly complements her.  \\"I tried to be your wife...I tried to become all that you expect of me, and if it was a failure, I don't give a...\\" she sings.<br /><br />I'm a Sinner - This is fun pop ditty that is unquestionably William Orbit-style.  The music is slightly reminiscent of \\"Beautiful Stranger\\".  Retro and playful.<br /><br />Love Spent - Oh my God...this song!  Probably my favorite on the album.  A tender-sounding that mostly laments how Madonna's financial situation hurts her love life.  \\"Love spent, yeah I'm love spent, wondering where the love went,\\" she sings.  The song has a dancey beat for most of it before completely changing for the second half, culminating in a beautiful climax.  Orbit's production here is masterful.<br /><br />Masterpiece - This song, written for her film W./E., is a beautiful ballad reminiscent of her Something to Remember days.  We haven't heard Madonna sound like this for a while, and it's fantastic.<br /><br />Falling Free - My second-favorite song on this album is also its quietest moment.  Madonna's vocals here are indescribably beautiful.  For the first time since Ray of Light, Madonna sings in that tender-yet-full voice we heard in Evita.  The song has soft orchestral arrangements and light piano with an electronica-flavored sound that borders on Celtic.  The song is so beautiful, especially near the end.<br /><br />Beautiful Killer - The first of the bonus discsongs, this song is pure retro.  Sounding like the kind of pop music you would hear in the 70's or early 80's, the song brims with guitar and strings.  I've never heard Madonna do anything like this, and I love it!<br /><br />I F***ed Up - A very personal song that wouldn't sound out of place if it were sung by someone like Alanis Morissette.  Madonna confesses her wrongdoings to a lover (could be Guy Ritchie, could be Jesus Luz).  Very apologetic, and not the norm for her.<br /><br />B-Day Song - *sigh* this is the album's weakest moment.  Madonna and M.I.A. sing a carefree song about having a good birthday.  The lyrics are trite, the music sounds unfinished and the song as a whole sounds like something recorded in 2 seconds.  It may work on some level, but when put in with the rest of MDNA is very forgettable.<br /><br />Best Friend - A very Hard Candy-ish hip-hop song, but unlike the songs on that album this one is also deeply personal.  There's no doubt that Madonna is singing about Guy Ritchie, and lamenting the loss of her \\"best friend\\" in Ritchie.  A strong track.<br /><br />Give Me All Your Luvin' (Party Rock Remix) - It's just a remix of GMAYL.  Nothing to shout about unless you're fan of LMFAO, who adds their own rap alongside that of Ms. Minaj.<br /><br />All in all, MDNA is a fantastic record.  On the whole, it can't really compare to any other Madonna album, and that is precisely why it wins.  Fresh, electronic soundscapes, pulsing beats, no-holds-barred lyrics...Madonna has never sounded more invigorating.  Even with its moments of weakness, you cannot go wrong with this album!

Part vamp, part shifter. I really enjoyed this little mix of supernaturals. My only wish for Charles would have been to see more of his `oldness' and more...something. Just, I wanted more. Hopefully in the next book, I will get that<br /><br />And when it comes to Sophia and Charles' relationship, I loved it! It was incredibly awkward for Sophia at times, embarrassing, and everything that a real romance is. There is no such thing as insta-love and I loved seeing their relationship transform. There were quite a few heated scenes that for sure made me realize that this is for people at least twenty.<br /><br />Ivory and Lauren, Sophia's best friends, were really interesting. Ivory's plot line actually broke my heart. I felt terrible for her. And Lauren, I hope there is more of in the next book.<br /><br />Final Thoughts: If there are any twenty something year olds out there looking for more than just YA, read this book. As much as I love me some YA, it was thoroughly enjoyable to read something a little more mature.

B0055PGUYU|2.0|The book's main point is just the basics of the science of behavior modification. According to the book habits are behavior that are routines that are cued by a stimulus and controlled by rewards. So, a cue triggers a routine that is rewarded. The behaviorists call the \\"cue\\" the antecedent, the \\"routine\\", the behavior and the \\"reward\\" the consequence, or ABC, Antecedent, Behavior, Consequence.<br /><br />The book tells interesting stories about the application of the ABC's of behavior modification in regard to various personal and cultural behaviors. The book is a veiled introduction to behaviorism and behavior modification. However, the book does not credit B. F. Skinner, the founder of the science of behavior at all or behaviorism or behavior modification science; therefore, my rating is 2 stars. How can the author write about behavior and be so ignorant of Skinner and behavior science? You can learn much more than this book has to offer if you want to really learn about habits (behavior) by reading the works of B. F. Skinner, Paul Chance and others behaviorists.

0765326353|5.0|First, the negatives.  Yes, even though I rated this book 5 stars, there are some - in my opinion.  Sanderson does repeat himself an awful lot.  Characters think the same thoughts over and over, chapter after chapter.  Past events are alluded to frequently.  Now, from my point of view and style of reading, this actually isn't a bad thing.  It takes me a long time to read a book, and I often am reading multiple books concurrently so that I usually read a couple of chapters of any given book during a day.  It's taken me over a month to read \\"The Way of Kings\\".  If I had read it more swiftly, being constantly reminded of things would annoy me more.  Sanderson HAS gotten better, and his repetition of material seems to be more organic to the thought process of the characters.  And let's be honest, it's realistic that characters would think the same thoughts - I know that there are certain events and thoughts that I reflect on each and every day.<br /><br />A second negative is that the book feels a touch disjointed in how it presents the world.  There are at least two distinct forms of Magic presented, possibly three.  Most of the world presented is rather fantastic - 'spren' which appear when certain emotions/sensations/physical events occur: fearspren when someone's afraid, honorspren when people win a great victory, windspren which show up when there's wind, rotspren at the site of a wound, etc... Most people can't see them, and it's not very clear what purpose they serve.  Knowing Sanderson, it will be revealed, but they feel like something of a distraction.  What's odd is that there's at least one chapter where it's obvious that part of the world doesn't have these beings, and is very different physically and biologically (plants act differently).  Right now, there's no reason why this is so.<br /><br />Those two negatives do not in any way bring down my rating of 5 stars.  For such a huge book, it didn't drag much.  The chapters were relatively short, and there were four main points of view with some interludes.  A couple of the interludes did seem somewhat pointless to me but perhaps will become more important in future books.  Unlike most books with different points of view, I found that I was interested in all of them to the same extent.  Each character went through quite a few moments of change in their lives, and Sanderson did a great job at fleshing out their past so that the reader knows why those characters are who they are.  Kaladin is easily the most fleshed out, a former spearman who once thought he would be a healer instead, and through most of the book is a slave in an army.  Someone else mentioned that the flashbacks seemed mere filler, as the reader could guess what happened to him well before it was revealed.  True enough, at least in one case.  But that didn't take away from their impact.  Knowing that something horrible happened, and even guessing what it might be, but getting only snippets at a time throughout the book, keeps a certain level of dread each time one of the flashbacks occurs.<br /><br />Sanderson's world building is very good.  Yes, there were a couple of apparent missteps as mentioned above, but they may be explained later.  One of the things I really liked was his treatment of men and women.  Women particularly have felt 'off' in some of his previous works.  In Elantris, the heroine felt far too modern for the society she was in.  In Mistborn, the heroine felt a bit too stereotyped.  Here, women are strong characters but there's a very good societal reason for it.  They're literate.  Men aren't.  They take down the messages, keep the histories, read books to men, etc.  It's strange, but somewhat believable as a separation of 'accepted' activities between the sexes.<br /><br />Sanderson's writing is 'transparent'.  I know it's repeated a lot, but it's simple and straightforward.  Not everyone likes this, and there are some books I much prefer a more 'literary' style.  I loved Daniel Abraham'sLong Price Quartet.  His writing style there was wordy, well constructed, and quite descriptive.  The books were also ~400 pages long, 1/3 of the size of this book.  Someone else compared two writing styles, suggesting that the more descriptive kind is more fun to read.  He even showed some helpful stats, where each sentence of a writer he considered much better was 2 1/2 times as long.  That would have made The Way of Kings 3000 pages long.  No thanks.  And the simple prose made the revelations spectacular.<br /><br />Ah yes, the revelations.  For anyone who read the Mistborn series the whole way through, you know that Sanderson did something very special.  There were certainly flaws - and bigger ones than in this book.  But the brief italicized passages at the beginning of each chapter were significant although the reader didn't find out the true significance until much later in each novel.  He also had a nice one involving an earring in which I literally said \\"Oh my god...\\" He repeats the same 'trope' here with italicized pieces before each chapter starts.  It won't give much away, they're basically 'last words' of people as they're dying.  The odd bit is the manner in which they're \\"recorded\\", with a notation like \\"9 Seconds before death.  Subject was a lighteyed woman.  Sample of particular note.\\"  I hope it won't spoil anyone's enjoyment to know that it's revealed what these actually are.  And I don't recall ever being stunned by a revelation like I was in this novel.  I actually had to pause when I realized what I was reading and regather myself before continuing on.  And that was just one of the 'reveals' that occurred.<br /><br />I could go on... Basically, this is the book I've been hoping Sanderson would write as he's shown immense creativity both in world building and in craft as a writer, in previous works.  He's not fancy, but the way he WEAVES a story, well, he does things I haven't seen done before.  It worries me that this is a ten volume (planned)series.  I'm not sure he can sustain this level of writing where others have failed.  But for now, this is certainly a fantastic start.

As I read I could see how much Card has influenced the current science fiction genre in the YA market.<br /><br />First of all, this was a very full-color read. Every world Ender is thrust into is fully mapped & fleshed out, from the playing fields of the game to the Bugger planets to the inner world of the computer game he plays periodically on his desk. Every character was given life & breathed on the pages. Card is a master at getting the psychology down right on his characters, all of which were multi-layered & most of which go through some sort of character growth throughout the book. Reading this book was like being in a simulator while the story played out in front of me because it was just so well written.<br /><br />Not a word of this book was wasted. Everything has meaning & a reason to be part of the story. Ender's struggle through battle school & life in general are fascinating. They reminded me, in part, of the struggles the protagonists go through to reach strength & leadership in The Maze Runner series, The Hunger Games series, & some of the Forest of Hands & Teeth series as well.<br /><br />Some might shy away from the book because it seems so sci-fi but believe me, this is not high science fiction. There aren't any technical science-y terms or complicated ideas to understand. It's the sweeping story of a little boy genius who is being groomed to win a war full of imaginative battle strategies & interesting people to get to know. Some might also shy away from it because it was written in the 1980s. The writing is not stilted or strange. If you were never told the publication date you could swear it was a contemporary book.<br /><br />That said, there were a couple things that I wasn't too fond of about this book. 0307719219|3.0|The authors of 'Why Nations Fail' contend that it is man-made political and economic institutions that underlie a nation's economic success or failure, not its culture or geography. As examples, they posit North and South Korea - the one thriving while the other barely escapes starvation. Another set of examples - Nogales in the U.S. vs. Nogales in Mexico. The authors contend that nations that became rich did so because their citizens overthrew the elites controlling power and created an environment where the great masses could invest and take advantage of economic opportunities.<br /><br />Spanish rule in South America was designed to force indigenous people's living standards down to a subsistence level and extract the excess. This was achieved by appropriating their lands, forcing them to work for low wages, imposing high taxes, and charging high prices for goods. Efforts to establish a similar system in Virginia failed because there were no valuable minerals and a low population density. Settlements in Virginia succeeded only when settlers were given land as an incentive, as well as all adult men a say in governance.<br /><br />The development climate in the U.S. at the prior turn of the century was much more favorable than in Mexico. There were 27,864 banks in the U.S. in 1914, with total assets of $27.3 billion. In 1910 Mexico, there were only 42, 2 of which controlled 60% of total banking assets. Thus there was little competition for loan business in Mexico, and loans were limited mostly to the privileged and came with high interest rates. U.S. banking started out similarly, but the system was forced to change by voters. Mexico, by contrast, was ruled by military fiat.<br /><br />Frontier lands in most of Latin American countries ere allocated to the politically powerful, and those with wealth and contacts. By contrast, broad access was granted to those lands in the U.S.<br /><br />Only in the 1990s did most Latin American countries become democracies, and even then they were mired in instability. Carlos Slim did not make his money by innovation like Bill Gates did, but through connections. His major coup was the acquisition of Telmex (Mexico's tele-communications monopoly) when it was privatized in 1990s via selling 51% of voting stock (only 20.4% of the total). Slim did not even make the highest bid but his consortium won and then used dividends (not cash) to pay for it. Protective laws and bureaucrats have served him since as protective barriers. Telmex charges among the highest usage fees in the world, according to the OECD. Slim made a foray into U.S. business, violated contracts, and was fined $454 million as a result. Even Bill Gates has felt the force of law in the U.S. - anti-trust law.<br /><br />The authors contend that the most common reason nations fail today is because they have relied on extractive institutions because this situation does not create incentives to save, invest, and innovate. Illustrations offered include Angola, Cameroon, Chad, Democratic Republic of Congo, Haiti, Liberia, Nepal, Sudan, and Zimbabwe. Another problem - lack of political centralization - eg. Afghanistan and Somalia, while Haiti lost its former centralized power.<br /><br />The authors' definition of extractive institutions (protecting the political and economic power of only a small elite that takes income from everyone else) is broader than might first appear - eg. not limited to natural resources. Example - Uzbekistan, which achieved independence via the breakup of the Soviet Union in 1991. At that time the stat-owned farms were broken up. However, regulations established what farmers could plant (35% cotton is the norm) and how much they could sell it for to the state (well below market price). Prior to 1991, 40% of the harvest was picked by machine, after the new price limits this was no longer affordable. Now about 75% is picked by school children for very minimal pay ($0.03/kilo, vs. a world price of $1.40) and with quotas of 20-60 kilos/day. Elections are rigged to keep the Karimov (former Communist official) family in power. The authors contend Egypt was largely run in the same manner.<br /><br />All the preceding is interesting, but seems quite slanted. For example - the Arab Emirates and Saudi Arabia are quite prosperous despite being run by extractive institutions. Their chapter titled 'You Can't Engineer Prosperity' flies in the face of success in China, Japan, Singapore, and South Korea. And they have no real explanation for China's outstanding successes over the past 30+ years, other than to predict it can't last. Perhaps, though there have been innumerable others before them making the same claim. Regardless, their logic also suggests that U.S. insitutions in their present form that create growing inequality of income and assets with a government dominated by influential donors, cannot last either.otas of 20-60 kilos/day. Elections are rigged to keep the Karimov (former Communist official) family in power. The authors contend Egypt was largely run in the same manner.<br /><br />All the preceding is interesting, but seems quite slanted. For example - the Arab Emirates and Saudi Arabia are quite prosperous despite being run by extractive institutions. Their chapter titled 'You Can't Engineer Prosperity' flies in the face of success in China, Japan, Singapore, and South Korea. And they have no real explanation for China's outstanding successes over the past 30+ years, other than to predict it can't last. Regardless, their logic also suggests that U.S. insitutions in their present form that create growing inequality of income and assets with a government dominated by influential donors, cannot last either.
