145162445X|5.0|Reading this book was a treat. Beforehand, I had only some layman's exposure to theories of cosmology that generally alluded to the nature of very prominent discoveries and reformulations by the \\"big shots\\" of physics throughout the years, and often times these contained dated material and discussed problems that have since been resolved by experiment (Like which of Alexander Friedman's solutions of Einstein's equations was relevant to our universe, for example). What's fantastic about this book is that it contains very recent material expounded by a physicist who is currently working on and contributing to these problems at the very forefront of cosmology. Clear as it has become that Dr. Krauss is himself an atheist and proponent of secular ideals, (concepts which I--for full disclosure--am very much in agreement with) it does not at all present a salient concern for the material discussed in this book. The most common criticisms of the book seem to be its failure to address the pure, philosophical nothing. This view was most recently advanced in the NY Times review by the philosopher of science, David Albert, who accused Krauss of trying to paint a picture of relativistic quantum field vacuum states as \\"not being physical stuff at all\\", which is something that I, personally, did not take away from Krauss' picture of quantum effects in current theories of cosmology. What it seems apparent that Krauss is doing, and what his critics fail to realize, is that Krauss is simply following the science where it leads; a concept which would also address Albert's repeating the phrase \\"Period. Case closed. End of story.\\" to patronize Krauss' for what he feels is and absolutist interpretation of contemporary cosmology. It would be a much less readable book if Krauss ended each sentence with \\"but, by the purest standards of epistemology, I can't say this for certain, and perhaps somewhere down the line we will be proven wrong and have to go back to the drawing board\\". Krauss does make note to mention that future experiments may prove some of his interpretations wrong (which should be taken as a truism). What he's doing with \\"nothing\\" is trying to find what this means in terms of our world. We don't have much experience observing our own universe begin, and given the severe difficulties in regards to quantum measurements, we still have much to learn about the arrangement of relativistic quantum mechanical fields. The pure, idealized, philosophical nothing may have nothing to do with a necessary state of the physical world, and Krauss is only concerned with the definitions of nothing that Physics can provide and he is only discussing potential candidates of such that we have empirically-supported reason to postulate upon. He does well to note that the contentions he advanced which would be necessitated by quantum gravity are by no means certain, as this is very far from a verified or complete theory. Ultimately Krauss recognizes that he is not addressing the philosophical nothing, because he is trying to find the nothing that actually corresponds to this world. So many of his critics seem to take the idealized nothing as a necessary precursor to all physical phenomenon, but this has not yet been observed or verified, which is what Physics tries its very best to do when explaining phenomena in the world.<br /><br />The nit-picky criticisms of David Albert, William Lane Craig, and others is really missing the point of this very accessible, layman's guide to contemporary cosmology. It contains great explanations of how we know the big bang occurred, how we know the age of our universe, how we know the universe is flat and why this is the most desired universe, how we know gravitational energy is cancelled out, how we know the universe is expanding, and most amazingly, how we now have reason to believe that quantum vacuum fluctuations contributed to the spontaneous density fluctuations that may have caused matter to collapse and form galactic clusters and galaxies and solar systems, where microscopic pockets of life may forever ride across the quickening expansion of the universe, bound together and saved from the void by local gravitational systems, and, if you're lucky enough, humanistic happiness.

The love triangle is resolved in a realistic way, if not satisfying to the readers who want a fairy-tale ending, and Katniss goes on to live the rest of her life battered, broken, and scarred, both physically and emotionally, yet still manages to find as much happiness as she can be expected to after everything she experienced.
